<script>
    window.addEventListener('load', function () {
        const announcementBar = document.getElementById('announcement-bar');
        const messages = [
            document.getElementById('message1'),
            document.getElementById('message2'),
            document.getElementById('message3'),
        ];

        // Retrieve the last shown message index from localStorage
        let currentIndex = parseInt(localStorage.getItem('currentMessageIndex') || '0', 10);

        // Hide all messages initially
        messages.forEach((message) => {
            message.style.display = 'none';
        });

        // Show the current message
        messages[currentIndex].style.display = 'flex';

        // Show the announcement bar
        setTimeout(() => {
            announcementBar.style.display = 'flex';
            document.body.classList.add('with-announcement');
        }, 10000); // 10-second delay

        // Update the index for the next page load
        currentIndex = (currentIndex + 1) % messages.length;
        localStorage.setItem('currentMessageIndex', currentIndex);

        // Close button functionality
        document.getElementById('close-announcement').addEventListener('click', function () {
            announcementBar.style.display = 'none';
            document.body.classList.remove('with-announcement');
        });
    });
</script>

{"@context":"https:\/\/schema.org\/","@type":"NewsArticle","@id":"https:\/\/cyble.com\/blog\/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise\/#newsarticle","url":"https:\/\/cyble.com\/blog\/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise\/","headline":"Cyble - New Ransomware Groups on the Rise","mainEntityOfPage":"https:\/\/cyble.com\/blog\/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise\/","datePublished":"2022-07-12T11:44:02-04:00","dateModified":"2022-07-12T12:03:01-04:00","description":"Cyble analyzes new ransomware families spotted in the wild led by notable examples such as LILITH, RedAlert, and 0Mega.","articleSection":"Malware","articleBody":"\"RedAlert,\" LILITH and 0mega leading a wave of Ransomware Campaigns        Ransomware is one of the most&nbsp;serious cybersecurity problems on the internet and possibly the most potent form of cybercrime plaguing organizations today. It has quickly become one of the most prominent and profitable types of malware for Threat Actors (TAs).    In a typical scenario, the ransomware infection starts with the TA gaining access to the target device. Depending on the type of ransomware, it can infect the entire operating system or encrypts individual files. The TAs will then typically demand payment from the victim for the decryption of their files.    Multiple new ransomware groups have surfaced recently, highlighting the adoption of ransomware attacks by TAs for monetary gains. A few of them include:    RedAlert Ransomware        RedAlert or N13V is a new ransomware strain that targets both Windows and Linux VMWare ESXi servers on corporate networks. The ransomware stops all running virtual machines and encrypts any file related to virtual machines, such as virtual disks. RedAlert Ransomware was named after a string with the same name in the ransom note, but threat actors named their campaign \"N13V\". RedAlert only accepts ransom payments in Monero, which is rather atypical for ransomware groups.    RedAlert ransomware has manual operations, which means TAs execute the ransomware after a complete takeover of the victim system. The ransomware binary provides various options to the TAs for performing pre-encryption operations such as stopping all virtual machines running on VMware ESXi, Asymmetric cryptography performance tests, etc.    The ransomware uses NTRUEncrypt public key encryption algorithm for encryption. The ransomware only targets log files (.log), swap files(.vswp), virtual disks(.vmdk), snapshot files (.vmsn) and memory files(.vmem) of VMware ESXi virtual machines. After encryption the ransomware appends a &nbsp;\u201c.crypt\u201d extension to the file.    The figure below shows the leak site of RedAlert ransomware.    Figure 1 \u2013 RedAlert Ransomware Leak Site        0mega Ransomware        Another new ransomware gang, \"0mega,\" is suspected of targeting organizations using Double Extortion techniques. The indicators of compromise of this ransomware strain are unavailable in the wild.    Still, as per researchers' comments, the ransomware appends the files with the \".0mega\" extension and creates ransom notes named&nbsp;\"DECRYPT-FILES.txt.\"    The figure below shows the 0mega Ransomware data leak site.    Figure 2 - 0mega Ransomware Leak Site        Lilith Ransomware        Ransomware operators now have another new tool at their disposal, named Lilith Ransomware. This threat can affect many file types and render them completely unusable.    Lilith ransomware encrypts files on the victim's machine and appends the extension of encrypted files&nbsp;as&nbsp;\".lilith.\" Afterward, a ransom note is created on the system to demand payment.    In this report, Cyble Research Labs conducts a deep analysis of Lilith&nbsp;ransomware to understand its behavior and infection mechanism.    Technical Analysis: Lilith Ransomware        Static analysis indicates that the Lilith ransomware file is a console-based x64 architecture executable written in C\/C++,&nbsp;as shown below.    Figure 3 - Static information of LILITH Ransomware        Upon execution, Lilith ransomware initially searches for a list of hardcoded processes in the file and terminates its execution if any of them are running on the target's machine. This step ensures that these processes do not block access to the files to be encrypted.    The below figure shows the APIs used to kill the process execution by the ransomware.    Figure 4 - APIs used to Terminate Process Execution        A full list of hardcoded process names is shown in the below figure.    Figure 5 - List of Processes for Termination        To identify the services running in the machine, the ransomware first calls \"OpenSCManagerA()\" API, which establishes a connection to the service control manager that gives the TAs access to the service control manager database.    Upon gaining access to this database, the following APIs() will be called:    OpenServiceA() \u2013 Opens the specified service.QueryServiceStatusEx() \u2013 Gets the status of the service.EnumDependentServiceA() \u2013 Retrieves the dependent services.ControlService() \u2013 takes control of the service for stopping.    If the \"OpenSCManagerA()\" API fails to get the handle to Service Control Manager (SCM), then the ransomware skips calling the below service-related APIs.    Figure 6 \u2013 Service-Related APIs        After that, the ransomware enumerates and gets the system drive information of the victim's machine by using the below APIs such as GetDriveTypeW(), FindFirstVolumeW(), and FindNextVolumeW().    Figure 7 \u2013 System Drive Related APIs        Before initiating the encryption process, the ransomware drops the ransom note&nbsp;in multiple folders with the file name \"Restore_Your_Files.txt.\" The ransomware creates a ransom note with the content shown in the figure below.    Figure 8 \u2013 Malware Writes Ransom Notes        The ransomware searches for files to encrypt on the local system by enumerating the file directories using FindFirstFileW() and&nbsp;FindNextFileW() API functions. It ignores the file extensions such as EXE, DLL, and SYS and excludes a list of directory and file names from the encryption process (Figure 9).    Interestingly, the exclusion list contains the filename \"ecdh_pub_k.bin,\" which stores the local public key of BABUK ransomware for file decryption.    Figure 9 \u2013 Exclusion List of Folder and File names        The malware uses cryptographic APIs such as CryptAcquireContextW() and CryptGenRandom() from ADVAPI32.dll to encrypt victims\u2019 files. The ransomware generates a random key with the function \"CryptGenRandom()\" and then encrypts the files using an encryption routine as shown below.    Figure 10 \u2013 Encryption Routine        The figure below shows the WriteFile operation and the original\/infected file content before and after encryption.    Figure 11 \u2013 WriteFile() Operation        Finally, the malware renames the encrypted file with the \".lilith\" extension and replaces it with the original file by using the \"MoveFileExW()\" API, as shown below.    Figure 12 \u2013 MoveFileExW() API        The below figure shows the encrypted files by Lilith ransomware after the successful infection of a victim's machine.    Figure 13 \u2013 Files Encrypted by Lilith Ransomware        In the dropped ransom note, victims are given three days to negotiate the price with the TAs for the decryption software. At the end of this deadline, the TAs threaten to begin leaking personal data if the ransom is not paid. &nbsp;    The ransom note also contains the poison ID for TOX communication and the Onion URL of the leak site page - shown in the figure below.    Figure 14 \u2013 Ransom Note        The figure below shows the Onion leak site home page of Lilith ransomware.    Figure 15 \u2013 Onion Leak Site        Conclusion        Ransomware groups continue to pose a severe threat to firms and individuals. Organizations need to stay ahead of the techniques used by&nbsp;TAs besides implementing the requisite security best&nbsp;practices and&nbsp;security controls.    Ransomware victims&nbsp;are at risk&nbsp;of&nbsp;losing valuable data as a result of such attacks, resulting in financial loss and lost productivity.&nbsp;If&nbsp;the victim&nbsp;is unable or unwilling to pay the ransom, the TAs may leak or sell this data online, compromising sensitive user data for businesses and individuals and resulting in a loss of reputation for the affected organization(s).&nbsp;    Throughout 2021 and 2022, we have observed record levels of ransomware activity. While notable examples of this are rebrands of existing groups, newer groups like LILITH, RedAlert, and 0mega are also proving to be potent threats.    Cyble&nbsp;Research Labs&nbsp;continuously monitors new ransomware campaigns and will keep our readers updated.    Our Recommendations        We have listed some essential cybersecurity best practices that create the first line of control against attackers. We recommend that our readers follow the best practices given below:    Safety Measures Needed to Prevent Ransomware Attacks    Conduct regular backup practices and keep those backups offline or in a separate network.Turn on the automatic software update feature on your computer, mobile, and other connected devices wherever possible and pragmatic.\u202fUse a reputed anti-virus and Internet security software package on your connected devices, including PC, laptop, and mobile.Refrain from opening untrusted links and email attachments without verifying their authenticity.    Users Should Take the Following Steps After the Ransomware Attack    Detach infected devices on the same network.Disconnect external storage devices if connected.Inspect system logs for suspicious events.    Impacts And Cruciality of Ransomware    Loss of Valuable data.Loss of the organization's reputation and integrity.Loss of the organization's sensitive business information.Disruption in organization operation.Financial loss. <style>

    * :focus { outline: 0; }
        .scroll {
    height: 1vh;
    font-size: 1rem;
    display: flex;
    justify-content: center;
    align-items: center;
  }
    
    .text-marquee {
    
      position: relative;
      display: flex;
      align-items: center;
      overflow-x: hidden;
    
    }
     
    .text-single {
      position: relative;
      white-space: pre;
      padding: 0;
      margin: 0;
      will-change: transform;
    background: #000;
    }
     
    .text {
    
      padding: 0;
      display: inline-block;
      margin-right: 2.8rem;
     font-family: 'Poppins',sans-serif; 
     font-weight: 400; 
     font-size: 14px;
     color: #fff;
    }
    .values:focus,.values:active{
        border:none;
        outline: 0;
    }
    .values .text{
        margin-left: 2px;
        margin-right: 3px;
    }
    .keys .text{
        margin-right: 5px;
        margin-left: 2.8rem;
        font-weight: bold;
    }
  
    .text-single a:last-child .text{
         padding-right: 2.8rem!important; 
    }
    
    
      .text.js-text:hover, .text.js-text:active, .text.js-text:focus{
         outline: 0 none;
      -moz-outline: 0 none;
      }  
      
    </style>
<script src="https://cdnjs.cloudflare.com/ajax/libs/jquery/3.6.0/jquery.min.js?ver<%=DateTime.Now.Ticks.ToString()%>" type="text/javascript"></script>
  
  
  
  <script src="https://unpkg.co/gsap@3/dist/gsap.min.js?ver<%=DateTime.Now.Ticks.ToString()%>" type="text/javascript"></script>
  
  
  
  <script src="https://unpkg.com/gsap@3/dist/Draggable.min.js?ver<%=DateTime.Now.Ticks.ToString()%>" type="text/javascript"></script>
  
  
  <script>
  document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function() {
      let loops = gsap.utils.toArray('.text-single').map((line, i) => {
          const links = line.querySelectorAll(".js-text"),
              tl = horizontalLoop(links, {
                  repeat: -1,
                  speed: 1 + i * 0.5,
                  draggable: true,
                  reversed: false,
                  paddingRight: parseFloat(gsap.getProperty(links[0], "marginRight", "px"))
              });
          links.forEach(link => {
              link.addEventListener("mouseenter", () => gsap.to(tl, {timeScale: 0, overwrite: true}));
              link.addEventListener("mouseleave", () => gsap.to(tl, {timeScale: i ? -1 : 1, overwrite: true}));
          });
      });
  
      let currentScroll = 0;
      let scrollDirection = 1;
  
      window.addEventListener("scroll", () => {
          let direction = (window.pageYOffset > currentScroll) ? 1 : -1;
          if (direction !== scrollDirection) {
              loops.forEach(tl => gsap.to(tl, {timeScale: direction, overwrite: true}));
              scrollDirection = direction;
          }
          currentScroll = window.pageYOffset;
      });
  
    function horizontalLoop(items, config) {
      items = gsap.utils.toArray(items);
      config = config || {};
      let tl = gsap.timeline({repeat: config.repeat, paused: config.paused, defaults: {ease: "none"}, onReverseComplete: () => tl.totalTime(tl.rawTime() + tl.duration() * 100)}),
          length = items.length,
          startX = items[0].offsetLeft,
          times = [],
          widths = [],
          xPercents = [],
          curIndex = 0,
          pixelsPerSecond = (config.speed || 1) * 100,
          snap = config.snap === false ? v => v : gsap.utils.snap(config.snap || 1), // some browsers shift by a pixel to accommodate flex layouts, so for example if width is 20% the first element's width might be 242px, and the next 243px, alternating back and forth. So we snap to 5 percentage points to make things look more natural
          populateWidths = () => items.forEach((el, i) => {
        widths[i] = parseFloat(gsap.getProperty(el, "width", "px"));
        xPercents[i] = snap(parseFloat(gsap.getProperty(el, "x", "px")) / widths[i] * 100 + gsap.getProperty(el, "xPercent"));
      }),
      getTotalWidth = () => items[length-1].offsetLeft + xPercents[length-1] / 100 * widths[length-1] - startX + items[length-1].offsetWidth * gsap.getProperty(items[length-1], "scaleX") + (parseFloat(config.paddingRight) || 0),
        totalWidth, curX, distanceToStart, distanceToLoop, item, i;
      populateWidths();
    gsap.set(items, { // convert "x" to "xPercent" to make things responsive, and populate the widths/xPercents Arrays to make lookups faster.
          xPercent: i => xPercents[i]
      });
      gsap.set(items, {x: 0});
      totalWidth = getTotalWidth();
      for (i = 0; i < length; i++) {
          item = items[i];
          curX = xPercents[i] / 100 * widths[i];
          distanceToStart = item.offsetLeft + curX - startX;
          distanceToLoop = distanceToStart + widths[i] * gsap.getProperty(item, "scaleX");
          tl.to(item, {xPercent: snap((curX - distanceToLoop) / widths[i] * 100), duration: distanceToLoop / pixelsPerSecond}, 0)
            .fromTo(item, {xPercent: snap((curX - distanceToLoop + totalWidth) / widths[i] * 100)}, {xPercent: xPercents[i], duration: (curX - distanceToLoop + totalWidth - curX) / pixelsPerSecond, immediateRender: false}, distanceToLoop / pixelsPerSecond)
            .add("label" + i, distanceToStart / pixelsPerSecond);
          times[i] = distanceToStart / pixelsPerSecond;
      }
      function toIndex(index, vars) {
          vars = vars || {};
          (Math.abs(index - curIndex) > length / 2) && (index += index > curIndex ? -length : length); // always go in the shortest direction
          let newIndex = gsap.utils.wrap(0, length, index),
              time = times[newIndex];
          if (time > tl.time() !== index > curIndex) { // if we're wrapping the timeline's playhead, make the proper adjustments
              vars.modifiers = {time: gsap.utils.wrap(0, tl.duration())};
              time += tl.duration() * (index > curIndex ? 1 : -1);
          }
          curIndex = newIndex;
          vars.overwrite = true;
          return tl.tweenTo(time, vars);
      }
      tl.next = vars => toIndex(curIndex+1, vars);
      tl.previous = vars => toIndex(curIndex-1, vars);
      tl.current = () => curIndex;
      tl.toIndex = (index, vars) => toIndex(index, vars);
    tl.updateIndex = () => curIndex = Math.round(tl.progress() * (items.length - 1));
      tl.times = times;
    tl.progress(1, true).progress(0, true); // pre-render for performance
    if (config.reversed) {
      tl.vars.onReverseComplete();
      tl.reverse();
    }
    if (config.draggable && typeof(Draggable) === "function") {
      let proxy = document.createElement("div"),
          wrap = gsap.utils.wrap(0, 1),
          ratio, startProgress, draggable, dragSnap, roundFactor,
          align = () => tl.progress(wrap(startProgress + (draggable.startX - draggable.x) * ratio)),
          syncIndex = () => tl.updateIndex();
      typeof(InertiaPlugin) === "undefined" && console.warn("InertiaPlugin required for momentum-based scrolling and snapping. https://greensock.com/club");
      draggable = Draggable.create(proxy, {
        trigger: items[0].parentNode,
        type: "x",
        onPress() {
          startProgress = tl.progress();
          tl.progress(0);
          populateWidths();
          totalWidth = getTotalWidth();
          ratio = 1 / totalWidth;
          dragSnap = totalWidth / items.length;
          roundFactor = Math.pow(10, ((dragSnap + "").split(".")[1] || "").length);
          tl.progress(startProgress);
        },
        onDrag: align,
        onThrowUpdate: align,
        inertia: true,
        snap: value => {
          let n = Math.round(parseFloat(value) / dragSnap) * dragSnap * roundFactor;
          return (n - n % 1) / roundFactor;
        },
        onRelease: syncIndex,
        onThrowComplete: () => gsap.set(proxy, {x: 0}) && syncIndex()
      })[0];
    }
    
      return tl;
  }
  });
  </script>

				</div>

					<h1 class="elementor-heading-title elementor-size-default">New Ransomware Groups on the Rise</h1>				</div>

					Cyble analyzes new ransomware families spotted in the wild led by notable examples such as LILITH, RedAlert, and 0Mega.				</div>

				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					
<h2 class="wp-block-heading">&#8220;RedAlert,&#8221; LILITH and 0mega leading a wave of Ransomware Campaigns</h2>



<p></p>



<p>Ransomware is one of the most&nbsp;serious cybersecurity problems on the internet and possibly the most potent form of cybercrime plaguing organizations today. It has quickly become one of the most prominent and profitable types of malware for Threat Actors (TAs).</p>



<p>In a typical scenario, the ransomware infection starts with the TA gaining access to the target device. Depending on the type of <a class="wpil_keyword_link" href="https://cyble.com/knowledge-hub/what-is-ransomware/" title="ransomware" data-wpil-keyword-link="linked" data-wpil-monitor-id="1089" target="_blank">ransomware</a>, it can infect the entire operating system or encrypts individual files. The TAs will then typically demand payment from the victim for the decryption of their files.</p><!-- This site is converting visitors into subscribers and customers with OptinMonster - https://optinmonster.com :: Campaign Title: Request demo blog -->
<div id="om-wlravxmwms40sunr2q0v-holder"></div>
<script>(function(d,u,ac){var s=d.createElement('script');s.type='text/javascript';s.src='https://a.omappapi.com/app/js/api.min.js';s.async=true;s.dataset.user=u;s.dataset.campaign=ac;d.getElementsByTagName('head')[0].appendChild(s);})(document,239265,'wlravxmwms40sunr2q0v');</script>
<!-- / OptinMonster -->



<p>Multiple new ransomware groups have surfaced recently, highlighting the adoption of ransomware attacks by TAs for monetary gains. A few of them include:</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-redalert-ransomware">RedAlert Ransomware</h2>



<p></p>



<p><a href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/new-redalert-ransomware-targets-windows-linux-vmware-esxi-servers/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">RedAlert</a> or N13V is a new ransomware strain that targets both Windows and Linux VMWare ESXi servers on corporate networks. The ransomware stops all running virtual machines and encrypts any file related to virtual machines, such as virtual disks. RedAlert Ransomware was named after a string with the same name in the ransom note, but threat actors named their campaign &#8220;N13V&#8221;. RedAlert only accepts ransom payments in Monero, which is rather atypical for ransomware groups.</p>



<p>RedAlert ransomware has manual operations, which means TAs execute the ransomware after a complete takeover of the victim system. The ransomware binary provides various options to the TAs for performing pre-encryption operations such as stopping all virtual machines running on VMware ESXi, Asymmetric cryptography performance tests, etc.</p>



<p>The ransomware uses NTRUEncrypt public key encryption algorithm for encryption. The ransomware only targets log files (.log), swap files(.vswp), virtual disks(.vmdk), snapshot files (.vmsn) and memory files(.vmem) of VMware ESXi virtual machines. After encryption the ransomware appends a &nbsp;“<em>.crypt[Random number]</em>” extension to the file.</p><div class="cyble-content_6" id="cyble-1045127091"><div style="position: relative; padding-bottom: 15%; height: 0; overflow: hidden; max-width: 100%; height: auto;">
<!-- Link wrapper around video -->

<p></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-0mega-ransomware">0mega Ransomware</h2>



<p></p>



<p>Another new ransomware gang, &#8220;0mega,&#8221; is suspected of targeting organizations using Double Extortion techniques. The indicators of compromise of this ransomware strain are unavailable in the wild.</p>



<p>Still, as per researchers&#8217; comments, the ransomware appends the files with the <em>&#8220;.0mega&#8221;</em> extension and creates ransom notes named&nbsp;&#8220;DECRYPT-FILES.txt.&#8221;</p>



<p>The figure below shows the 0mega Ransomware data leak site.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-large"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1024" height="308" data-attachment-id="11828" class="wp-element-caption">Figure 2 &#8211; 0mega Ransomware Leak Site</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-lilith-ransomware">Lilith Ransomware</h2>



<p></p>



<p>Ransomware operators now have another new tool at their disposal, named Lilith Ransomware. This threat can affect many file types and render them completely unusable.</p>



<p>Lilith ransomware encrypts files on the victim&#8217;s machine and appends the extension of encrypted files&nbsp;as&nbsp;<em>&#8220;.lilith.&#8221;</em> Afterward, a ransom note is created on the system to demand payment.</p>



<p>In this report, Cyble Research Labs conducts a deep analysis of Lilith&nbsp;ransomware to understand its behavior and infection mechanism.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-technical-analysis-lilith-ransomware">Technical Analysis: Lilith Ransomware</h3>



<p></p>



<p>Static analysis indicates that the Lilith ransomware file is a console-based x64 architecture executable written in C/C++,&nbsp;as shown below.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-full"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="723" height="395" data-attachment-id="11829" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-3-static-information-of-lilith-ransomware/" data-orig-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-3-Static-information-of-LILITH-Ransomware.jpg?fit=723%2C395&amp;ssl=1" data-orig-size="723,395" data-comments-opened="0" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Figure-3-Static-information-of-LILITH-Ransomware" data-image-description="" data-image-caption="" data-medium-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-3-Static-information-of-LILITH-Ransomware.jpg?fit=300%2C164&amp;ssl=1" data-large-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-3-Static-information-of-LILITH-Ransomware.jpg?fit=723%2C395&amp;ssl=1" src="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-3-Static-information-of-LILITH-Ransomware.jpg?resize=723%2C395&#038;ssl=1" alt="LILITH Ransomware" class="wp-image-11829" title="New Ransomware Groups on the Rise 18" srcset="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-3-Static-information-of-LILITH-Ransomware.jpg?w=723&amp;ssl=1 723w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-3-Static-information-of-LILITH-Ransomware.jpg?resize=300%2C164&amp;ssl=1 300w" sizes="(max-width: 723px) 100vw, 723px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 3 &#8211; Static information of LILITH Ransomware</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<p>Upon execution, Lilith ransomware initially searches for a list of hardcoded processes in the file and terminates its execution if any of them are running on the target&#8217;s machine. This step ensures that these processes do not block access to the files to be encrypted.</p>



<p>The below figure shows the APIs used to kill the process execution by the ransomware.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-large"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1024" height="166" data-attachment-id="11830" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-4-apis-used-to-terminate-process-execution/" data-orig-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution.jpg?fit=1032%2C167&amp;ssl=1" data-orig-size="1032,167" data-comments-opened="0" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution" data-image-description="" data-image-caption="" data-medium-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution.jpg?fit=300%2C49&amp;ssl=1" data-large-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution.jpg?fit=1024%2C166&amp;ssl=1" src="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution-1024x166.jpg?resize=1024%2C166&#038;ssl=1" alt="LILITH Ransomware" class="wp-image-11830" title="New Ransomware Groups on the Rise 19" srcset="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution.jpg?resize=1024%2C166&amp;ssl=1 1024w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution.jpg?resize=300%2C49&amp;ssl=1 300w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution.jpg?resize=768%2C124&amp;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-4-APIs-used-to-Terminate-Process-Execution.jpg?w=1032&amp;ssl=1 1032w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 4 &#8211; APIs used to Terminate Process Execution</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<p>A full list of hardcoded process names is shown in the below figure.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-full"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="532" height="518" data-attachment-id="11831" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-5-list-of-processes-for-termination/" data-orig-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-5-List-of-Processes-for-Termination.jpg?fit=532%2C518&amp;ssl=1" data-orig-size="532,518" data-comments-opened="0" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Figure-5-List-of-Processes-for-Termination" data-image-description="" data-image-caption="" data-medium-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-5-List-of-Processes-for-Termination.jpg?fit=300%2C292&amp;ssl=1" data-large-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-5-List-of-Processes-for-Termination.jpg?fit=532%2C518&amp;ssl=1" src="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-5-List-of-Processes-for-Termination.jpg?resize=532%2C518&#038;ssl=1" alt="LILITH Ransomware" class="wp-image-11831" title="New Ransomware Groups on the Rise 20" srcset="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-5-List-of-Processes-for-Termination.jpg?w=532&amp;ssl=1 532w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-5-List-of-Processes-for-Termination.jpg?resize=300%2C292&amp;ssl=1 300w" sizes="(max-width: 532px) 100vw, 532px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 5 &#8211; List of Processes for Termination</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<p>To identify the services running in the machine, the ransomware first calls <em>&#8220;OpenSCManagerA()&#8221;</em> API, which establishes a connection to the service control manager that gives the TAs access to the service control manager database.</p>



<p>Upon gaining access to this database, the following APIs() will be called:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list"><li><em>OpenServiceA()</em> – Opens the specified service.</li><li><em>QueryServiceStatusEx()</em> – Gets the status of the service.</li><li><em>EnumDependentServiceA()</em> – Retrieves the dependent services.</li><li><em>ControlService()</em> – takes control of the service for stopping.</li></ul>



<p>If the <em>&#8220;OpenSCManagerA()&#8221;</em> API fails to get the handle to Service Control Manager (SCM), then the ransomware skips calling the below service-related APIs.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-large"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1024" height="135" data-attachment-id="11832" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-6-service-related-apis/" data-orig-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-6-%E2%80%93-Service-Related-APIs.jpg?fit=1030%2C136&amp;ssl=1" data-orig-size="1030,136" data-comments-opened="0" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Figure-6-–-Service-Related-APIs" data-image-description="" data-image-caption="" data-medium-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-6-%E2%80%93-Service-Related-APIs.jpg?fit=300%2C40&amp;ssl=1" data-large-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-6-%E2%80%93-Service-Related-APIs.jpg?fit=1024%2C135&amp;ssl=1" src="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-6-%E2%80%93-Service-Related-APIs.jpg?resize=1024%2C135&#038;ssl=1" alt="LILITH Ransomware" class="wp-image-11832" title="New Ransomware Groups on the Rise 21" srcset="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-6-%E2%80%93-Service-Related-APIs.jpg?resize=1024%2C135&amp;ssl=1 1024w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-6-%E2%80%93-Service-Related-APIs.jpg?resize=300%2C40&amp;ssl=1 300w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-6-%E2%80%93-Service-Related-APIs.jpg?resize=768%2C101&amp;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-6-%E2%80%93-Service-Related-APIs.jpg?w=1030&amp;ssl=1 1030w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 6 – Service-Related APIs</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<p>After that, the ransomware enumerates and gets the system drive information of the victim&#8217;s machine by using the below APIs such as <em>GetDriveTypeW(), FindFirstVolumeW(),</em> and <em>FindNextVolumeW()</em>.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-full"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="997" height="180" data-attachment-id="11833" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-7-system-drive-related-apis/" data-orig-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-7-%E2%80%93-system-Drive-Related-APIs.jpg?fit=997%2C180&amp;ssl=1" data-orig-size="997,180" data-comments-opened="0" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Figure-7-–-system-Drive-Related-APIs" data-image-description="" data-image-caption="" data-medium-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-7-%E2%80%93-system-Drive-Related-APIs.jpg?fit=300%2C54&amp;ssl=1" Ransomware Groups on the Rise 22" srcset="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-7-%E2%80%93-system-Drive-Related-APIs.jpg?w=997&amp;ssl=1 997w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-7-%E2%80%93-system-Drive-Related-APIs.jpg?resize=768%2C139&amp;ssl=1 768w" sizes="(max-width: 997px) 100vw, 997px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 7 – System Drive Related APIs</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<p>Before initiating the encryption process, the ransomware drops the ransom note&nbsp;in multiple folders with the file name <em>&#8220;Restore_Your_Files.txt.&#8221; </em>The ransomware creates a ransom note with the content shown in the figure below.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-full"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1008" height="395" data-attachment-id="11834" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-8-malware-writes-ransom-notes/" data-image-title="Figure-8-–-Malware-Writes-Ransom-Notes" data-image-description="" data-image-caption="" data-medium-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-8-%E2%80%93-Malware-Writes-Ransom-Notes.jpg?fit=300%2C118&amp;ssl=1" Ransomware Groups on the Rise 23" srcset="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-8-%E2%80%93-Malware-Writes-Ransom-Notes.jpg?w=1008&amp;ssl=1 1008w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-8-%E2%80%93-Malware-Writes-Ransom-Notes.jpg?resize=300%2C118&amp;ssl=1 300w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-8-%E2%80%93-Malware-Writes-Ransom-Notes.jpg?resize=768%2C301&amp;ssl=1 768w" sizes="(max-width: 1008px) 100vw, 1008px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 8 – Malware Writes Ransom Notes</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<p>The ransomware searches for files to encrypt on the local system by enumerating the file directories using <em>FindFirstFileW()</em> and&nbsp;<em>FindNextFileW()</em> API functions. It ignores the file extensions such as EXE, DLL, and SYS and excludes a list of directory and file names from the encryption process (Figure 9).</p>



<p>Interestingly, the exclusion list contains the filename <em>&#8220;ecdh_pub_k.bin,&#8221;</em> which stores the local public key of BABUK ransomware for file decryption.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-full"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="631" height="549" data-attachment-id="11835" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-9-exclusion-list-of-folder-and-file-names/" data-comments-opened="0" https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-9-%E2%80%93-Exclusion-List-of-Folder-and-File-names.jpg?resize=300%2C261&amp;ssl=1 300w" sizes="(max-width: 631px) 100vw, 631px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 9 – Exclusion List of Folder and File names</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<p>The <a class="wpil_keyword_link" href="https://cyble.com/knowledge-hub/what-is-malware/" title="malware" data-wpil-keyword-link="linked" data-wpil-monitor-id="420" target="_blank">malware</a> uses cryptographic APIs such as <em>CryptAcquireContextW()</em> and <em>CryptGenRandom()</em> from ADVAPI32.dll to encrypt victims’ files. The ransomware generates a random key with the function <em>&#8220;CryptGenRandom()&#8221; </em>and then encrypts the files using an encryption routine as shown below.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-full"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="972" height="581" data-attachment-id="11837" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-10-encryption-routine/" data-orig-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-10-%E2%80%93-Encryption-Routine.jpg?fit=972%2C581&amp;ssl=1" data-orig-size="972,581" data-comments-opened="0" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Figure-10-–-Encryption-Routine" data-image-description="" data-image-caption="" data-medium-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-10-%E2%80%93-Encryption-Routine.jpg?fit=300%2C179&amp;ssl=1" data-large-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-10-%E2%80%93-Encryption-Routine.jpg?fit=972%2C581&amp;ssl=1" src="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-10-%E2%80%93-Encryption-Routine.jpg?resize=972%2C581&#038;ssl=1" alt="LILITH Ransomware" class="wp-image-11837" title="New Ransomware Groups on the Rise 25" srcset="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-10-%E2%80%93-Encryption-Routine.jpg?w=972&amp;ssl=1 972w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-10-%E2%80%93-Encryption-Routine.jpg?resize=300%2C179&amp;ssl=1 300w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-10-%E2%80%93-Encryption-Routine.jpg?resize=768%2C459&amp;ssl=1 768w" sizes="(max-width: 972px) 100vw, 972px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 10 – Encryption Routine</figcaption></figure>



<p></p>



<p>The figure below shows the WriteFile operation and the original/infected file content before and after encryption.</p>

<p></p>



<p>Finally, the malware renames the encrypted file with the <em>&#8220;.lilith&#8221;</em> extension and replaces it with the original file by using the <em>&#8220;MoveFileExW()&#8221;</em> API, as shown below.</p>

<p></p>



<p>In the dropped ransom note, victims are given three days to negotiate the price with the TAs for the decryption software. At the end of this deadline, the TAs threaten to begin leaking personal data if the ransom is not paid. &nbsp;</p>



<p>The ransom note also contains the poison ID for TOX communication and the Onion URL of the leak site page &#8211; shown in the figure below.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-full"><img data-recalc-dims="1" loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="975" height="568" data-attachment-id="11842" data-permalink="https://cyble.com/blog/new-ransomware-groups-on-the-rise/figure-14-ransom-note/" data-orig-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-14-%E2%80%93-Ransom-Note.jpg?fit=975%2C568&amp;ssl=1" data-orig-size="975,568" data-comments-opened="0" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Figure-14-–-Ransom-Note" data-image-description="" data-image-caption="" data-medium-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-14-%E2%80%93-Ransom-Note.jpg?fit=300%2C175&amp;ssl=1" data-large-file="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-14-%E2%80%93-Ransom-Note.jpg?fit=975%2C568&amp;ssl=1" src="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-14-%E2%80%93-Ransom-Note.jpg?resize=975%2C568&#038;ssl=1" alt="LILITH Ransomware" class="wp-image-11842" title="New Ransomware Groups on the Rise 29" srcset="https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-14-%E2%80%93-Ransom-Note.jpg?w=975&amp;ssl=1 975w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-14-%E2%80%93-Ransom-Note.jpg?resize=300%2C175&amp;ssl=1 300w, https://i0.wp.com/cyble.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/Figure-14-%E2%80%93-Ransom-Note.jpg?resize=768%2C447&amp;ssl=1 768w" sizes="(max-width: 975px) 100vw, 975px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Figure 14 – Ransom Note</figcaption></figure>

<p></p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-conclusion">Conclusion</h2>



<p></p>



<p>Ransomware groups continue to pose a severe threat to firms and individuals. Organizations need to stay ahead of the techniques used by&nbsp;TAs besides implementing the requisite security best&nbsp;practices and&nbsp;security controls.</p>



<p>Ransomware victims&nbsp;are at risk&nbsp;of&nbsp;losing valuable data as a result of such attacks, resulting in financial loss and lost productivity.&nbsp;If&nbsp;the victim&nbsp;is unable or unwilling to pay the ransom, the TAs may leak or sell this data online, compromising sensitive user data for businesses and individuals and resulting in a loss of reputation for the affected organization(s).&nbsp;</p>



<p>Throughout 2021 and 2022, we have observed record levels of ransomware activity. While notable examples of this are rebrands of existing groups, newer groups like LILITH, RedAlert, and 0mega are also proving to be potent threats.</p>



<p>Cyble&nbsp;Research Labs&nbsp;continuously monitors new ransomware campaigns and will keep our readers updated.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-our-recommendations">Our Recommendations</h2>



<p></p>



<p>We have listed some essential cybersecurity best practices that create the first line of control against attackers. We recommend that our readers follow the best practices given below:</p>



<p><strong>Safety Measures Needed to Prevent Ransomware Attacks</strong></p>



<ul class="wp-block-list"><li>Conduct regular backup practices and keep those backups offline or in a separate network.</li><li>Turn on the automatic software update feature on your computer, mobile, and other connected devices wherever possible and pragmatic. </li><li>Use a reputed anti-virus and Internet security software package on your connected devices, including PC, laptop, and mobile.</li><li>Refrain from opening untrusted links and email attachments without verifying their authenticity.</li></ul>



<p><strong>Users Should Take the Following Steps After the Ransomware Attack</strong></p>



<ul class="wp-block-list"><li>Detach infected devices on the same network.</li><li>Disconnect external storage devices if connected.</li><li>Inspect system logs for suspicious events.</li></ul>



<p><strong>Impacts And Cruciality of Ransomware</strong></p>



<ul class="wp-block-list"><li>Loss of Valuable data.</li><li>Loss of the organization&#8217;s reputation and integrity.</li><li>Loss of the organization&#8217;s sensitive business information.</li><li>Disruption in organization operation.</li><li>Financial loss.</li></ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-mitre-att-ck-techniques">MITRE ATT&amp;CK® Techniques</h2>



<p></p>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table><tbody><tr><td><strong>Tactic</strong></td><td><strong>Technique ID</strong></td><td><strong>Technique Name</strong></td></tr><tr><td>Execution</td><td><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1204/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">T1204</a></td><td>User Execution</td></tr><tr><td>Discovery</td><td><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1012/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">T1012</a> <br><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1082/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">T1082</a><br><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1083" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">T1083</a></td><td>Query Registry <br>System Information Discovery <br>File and Directory Discovery</td></tr><tr><td>Defense Evasion<strong></strong></td><td><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1027" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">T1027</a></td><td>Obfuscated Files or Information</td></tr><tr><td>Impact</td><td><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1486/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">T1486</a></td><td>Data Encrypted for Impact</td></tr></tbody></table></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-indicator-of-compromise-iocs">Indicator Of Compromise (IOCs)</h2>



<figure class="wp-block-table"><table><tbody><tr><td><strong>Indicators</strong></td><td><strong>Indicator<br>Type</strong></td><td><strong>Description</strong></td></tr><tr><td>b7a182db3ba75e737f75bda1bc76331a<strong></strong> cf0fe28214ad4106c48ec5867327319eaa82b3c3<strong></strong> f3caa040efb298878b99f883a898f76d92554e07a8958e90ff70e7ff3cfabdf5</td><td>MD5 SHA1 Sha256</td><td>LILITH Ransomware x64 EXE file</td></tr><tr><td>f2fa9a3ce883a7f5b43ba5c9ff7bdf75 da6a7e9d39f6a9c802bbd1ce60909de2b6e2a2aa 039e1765de1cdec65ad5e49266ab794f8e5642adb 0bdeb78d8c0b77e8b34ae09</td><td>MD5 SHA1 Sha256</td><td>RedAlert Ransomware Linux file (elf)</td></tr></tbody></table></figure>

<script>
   let autoHideTimeout;

document.addEventListener('scroll', function() {
    var scrollPosition = window.scrollY || document.documentElement.scrollTop;
    var documentHeight = document.documentElement.scrollHeight - document.documentElement.clientHeight;

    // Calculate scroll percentage
    var scrollPercentage = (scrollPosition / documentHeight) * 100;

    // Check if the screen width is greater than 768px (for non-mobile devices)
    if (window.innerWidth > 768) {
        // Only activate the slider when scroll is between 60% and 100%
        if (scrollPercentage >= 60 && scrollPercentage <= 100) {
            // Show the slider by sliding it in
            document.getElementById('rightSlider').classList.add('active');

            // Clear any previous auto-hide timer
            clearTimeout(autoHideTimeout);

            // Set a 5-second timer to auto-slide the slider back out
            autoHideTimeout = setTimeout(function() {
                document.getElementById('rightSlider').classList.remove('active');
            }, 5000); // 5000 milliseconds = 5 seconds
        } else {
            // If not within 60% - 100% range, hide the slider
            document.getElementById('rightSlider').classList.remove('active');
        }
    }
});

document.getElementById('rightSlider').addEventListener('mouseover', function() {
    // On mouseover, keep the slider active and clear the auto-hide timer
    clearTimeout(autoHideTimeout);
    this.classList.add('active');
});

document.getElementById('rightSlider').addEventListener('mouseleave', function() {
    // On mouse leave, start the auto-hide timer again for 5 seconds
    clearTimeout(autoHideTimeout);
    autoHideTimeout = setTimeout(function() {
        document.getElementById('rightSlider').classList.remove('active');
    }, 5000); // 5000 milliseconds = 5 seconds
});

</script>				</div>

					<script type='text/javascript'>
				const lazyloadRunObserver = () => {
					const lazyloadBackgrounds = document.querySelectorAll( `.e-con.e-parent:not(.e-lazyloaded)` );
					const lazyloadBackgroundObserver = new IntersectionObserver( ( entries ) => {
						entries.forEach( ( entry ) => {
							if ( entry.isIntersecting ) {
								let lazyloadBackground = entry.target;
								if( lazyloadBackground ) {
									lazyloadBackground.classList.add( 'e-lazyloaded' );
								}
								lazyloadBackgroundObserver.unobserve( entry.target );
							}
						});
					}, { rootMargin: '200px 0px 200px 0px' } );
					lazyloadBackgrounds.forEach( ( lazyloadBackground ) => {
						lazyloadBackgroundObserver.observe( lazyloadBackground );
					} );
				};
				const events = [
					'DOMContentLoaded',
					'elementor/lazyload/observe',
				];
				events.forEach( ( event ) => {
					document.addEventListener( event, lazyloadRunObserver );
				} );
			</script>
